       <tr class="noprint">
        <td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background:#CCF; font-weight:bold">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wikipe-tan_face.svg">
          <img alt="Wikipe-tan face.svg" class="noviewer" data-file-height="440" data-file-width="440" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Wikipe-tan_face.svg/16px-Wikipe-tan_face.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Wikipe-tan_face.svg/24px-Wikipe-tan_face.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Wikipe-tan_face.svg/32px-Wikipe-tan_face.svg.png 2x" width="16"/>
         </a>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Portal:Anime_and_Manga" title="Portal:Anime and Manga">
          Anime and Manga portal
         </a>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       <i>
        <b>
         Sailor Moon
        </b>
       </i>
       <span style="font-weight: normal">
        (
        <span lang="ja">
         美少女戦士セーラームーン
        </span>
        <span style="display:none">
         ,
        </span>
        <i>
         Bishōjo Senshi Sērā Mūn
        </i>
        , originally translated as
        <i>
         <b>
          Pretty Soldier Sailor Moon
         </b>
        </i>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Takeuchi_1-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Takeuchi-1">
          [1]
         </a>
        </sup>
        and later as
        <i>
         <b>
          Pretty Guardian Sailor Moon
         </b>
        </i>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-2">
          [2]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-3">
          [3]
         </a>
        </sup>
        )
       </span>
       is a Japanese
       <a href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Djo_manga" title="Shōjo manga">
        <i>
         shōjo
        </i>
        manga
       </a>
       series by
       <a href="/wiki/Naoko_Takeuchi" title="Naoko Takeuchi">
        Naoko Takeuchi
       </a>
       . It was originally serialized in
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Nakayoshi" title="Nakayoshi">
         Nakayoshi
        </a>
       </i>
       from 1991 to 1997; the 52 individual chapters were published in 18
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Tank%C5%8Dbon" title="Tankōbon">
         tankōbon
        </a>
       </i>
       volumes. The series follows the adventures of a schoolgirl named
       <a href="/wiki/Sailor_Moon_(character)" title="Sailor Moon (character)">
        Usagi Tsukino
       </a>
       as she transforms into Sailor Moon to search for a magical artifact, the "Legendary Silver Crystal"
       <span style="font-weight: normal">
        (
        <span lang="ja">
         「幻の銀水晶」
        </span>
        <span style="display:none">
         ,
        </span>
        <i>
         Maboroshi no Ginzuishō
        </i>
        , lit. "Phantom Silver Crystal")
       </span>
       . She leads a diverse group of comrades, the Sailor Soldiers
       <span style="font-weight: normal">
        (
        <span lang="ja">
         セーラー戦士
        </span>
        <span style="display:none">
         ,
        </span>
        <i>
         Sērā Senshi
        </i>
        )
       </span>
       —Sailor Guardians in later editions—as they battle against villains to prevent the theft of the Silver Crystal and the destruction of the
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">
        Solar System
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The manga was adapted into an anime series produced by
       <a href="/wiki/Toei_Animation" title="Toei Animation">
        Toei Animation
       </a>
       and broadcast in Japan from 1992 to 1997.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DVD‐COLLECTION_Vol.1_4-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-DVD‐COLLECTION_Vol.1-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DVD‐COLLECTION_Vol.2_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-DVD‐COLLECTION_Vol.2-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Toei also developed three animated feature films, a television special, and three short films based on the anime. A live-action television adaptation,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Pretty_Guardian_Sailor_Moon_(live-action_series)" title="Pretty Guardian Sailor Moon (live-action series)">
         Pretty Guardian Sailor Moon
        </a>
       </i>
       , aired from 2003 to 2004, and a
       <a href="/wiki/Reboot_(fiction)" title="Reboot (fiction)">
        reboot
       </a>
       of the anime series,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Sailor_Moon_Crystal" title="Sailor Moon Crystal">
         Sailor Moon Crystal
        </a>
       </i>
       , began
       <a href="/wiki/Simulcast" title="Simulcast">
        simulcasting
       </a>
       in 2014. The manga series was licensed for an English language release by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kodansha_Comics" title="Kodansha Comics">
        Kodansha Comics
       </a>
       in North America, and in Australia and New Zealand by Random House Australia. The entire anime series has been licensed by
       <a href="/wiki/Viz_Media" title="Viz Media">
        Viz Media
       </a>
       for an English language release in North America and by
       <a href="/wiki/Madman_Entertainment" title="Madman Entertainment">
        Madman Entertainment
       </a>
       in Australia and New Zealand.
      </p>
      <p>
       Since its release,
       <i>
        Pretty Soldier Sailor Moon
       </i>
       has received acclaim, with praise for its art, characterization and humor. The series has sold over 35 million copies worldwide, making it one of the best-selling
       <i>
        shojo
       </i>
       series.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Plot">
        Plot
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sailor_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Plot">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Sailor_Moon_characters" title="List of Sailor Moon characters">
        List of Sailor Moon characters
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Minato,_Tokyo" title="Minato, Tokyo">
        Minato, Tokyo
       </a>
       , a
       <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Japan" title="Education in Japan">
        middle-school
       </a>
       student named
       <a href="/wiki/Sailor_Moon_(character)" title="Sailor Moon (character)">
        Usagi Tsukino
       </a>
       befriends
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luna_(Sailor_Moon)" title="Luna (Sailor Moon)">
        Luna
       </a>
       , a talking
       <a href="/wiki/Black_cat" title="Black cat">
        black cat
       </a>
       who gives her a magical brooch enabling her to become Sailor Moon: a soldier destined to save Earth from the forces of evil. Luna and Usagi assemble a team of fellow Sailor Soldiers to find their
       <a href="/wiki/Princess" title="Princess">
        princess
       </a>
       and the Silver Crystal. They encounter the studious Ami Mizuno, who awakens as
       <a href="/wiki/Sailor_Mercury" title="Sailor Mercury">
        Sailor Mercury
       </a>
       ; Rei Hino, a local
       <a href="/wiki/Miko" title="Miko">
        shrine maiden
       </a>
       who awakens as
       <a href="/wiki/Sailor_Mars" title="Sailor Mars">
        Sailor Mars
       </a>
       ; Makoto Kino, a tall transfer student who awakens as
       <a href="/wiki/Sailor_Jupiter" title="Sailor Jupiter">
        Sailor Jupiter
       </a>
       ; and Minako Aino, a young aspiring
       <a href="/wiki/Japanese_idol" title="Japanese idol">
        idol
       </a>
       who awakens as
       <a href="/wiki/Sailor_Venus" title="Sailor Venus">
        Sailor Venus
       </a>
       , accompanied by her talking feline companion
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artemis_(Sailor_Moon)" title="Artemis (Sailor Moon)">
        Artemis
       </a>
       . Additionally, they encounter Mamoru Chiba, a high-school student who assists them on occasion as
       <a href="/wiki/Tuxedo_Mask" title="Tuxedo Mask">
        Tuxedo Mask
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In the first arc, the group battles the
       <a href="/wiki/Dark_Kingdom" title="Dark Kingdom">
        Dark Kingdom
       </a>
       . Led by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen_Beryl" title="Queen Beryl">
        Queen Beryl
       </a>
       , a team of generals—the Four Kings of Heaven
       <span style="font-weight: normal">
        (
        <span lang="ja">
         四天王
        </span>
        <span style="display:none">
         ,
        </span>
        <i>
         Shiten'ō
        </i>
        , lit. "Four Heavenly Kings")
       </span>
       —attempt to find the Silver Crystal to free an imprisoned, evil entity called
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen_Metaria" title="Queen Metaria">
        Queen Metaria
       </a>
       . Usagi and her team discover that in their previous lives they were members of the ancient moon kingdom called Silver Millennium. The Dark Kingdom waged war against them, resulting in the destruction of the moon kingdom. Its ruler
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen_Serenity" title="Queen Serenity">
        Queen Serenity
       </a>
       later sent her daughter Princess Serenity, her protectors the Sailor Soldiers, their feline advisers Luna and Artemis, and the princess's true love Prince Endymion into the future to be reborn through the power of the Silver Crystal. The team recognize Usagi as the reincarnated Serenity and Mamoru as Endymion. The Soldiers kill the Four Kings, who turn out to have been Endymion's guardians who defected in their past lives. In a final confrontation with the Dark Kingdom, Minako kills Queen Beryl; she and the other Soldiers then sacrifice their lives in an attempt to destroy Queen Metaria. Using the Silver Crystal, Usagi succeeds in killing Metaria and resurrects her friends.
      </p>
      <p>
       At the beginning of the second arc, Usagi and Mamoru's daughter
       <a href="/wiki/Chibiusa" title="Chibiusa">
        Chibiusa
       </a>
       arrives from the future to find the Silver Crystal. As a result, the Soldiers encounter
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Death_Phantom" title="Death Phantom">
        Wiseman
       </a>
       and his
       <a href="/wiki/Black_Moon_Clan" title="Black Moon Clan">
        Black Moon Clan
       </a>
       , who are pursuing her. Chibiusa takes the Soldiers to the future city Crystal Tokyo, where her parents rule as Neo-Queen Serenity and King Endymion. During their journey they meet
       <a href="/wiki/Sailor_Pluto" title="Sailor Pluto">
        Sailor Pluto
       </a>
       , guardian of the Time-Space Door. Pluto stops the Clan's ruler
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prince_Demand" title="Prince Demand">
        Prince Demand
       </a>
       from destroying the
       <a href="/wiki/Spacetime" title="Spacetime">
        spacetime continuum
       </a>
       , leading to her death. Chibiusa later awakens as a Soldier—Sailor Chibi Moon—and helps Usagi kill Wiseman's true form, Death Phantom.
      </p>
      <p>
       The third arc revolves around a group of lifeforms called the
       <a href="/wiki/Death_Busters" title="Death Busters">
        Death Busters
       </a>
       , created by Professor Soichi Tomoe, who seek to transport the entity
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pharaoh_90" title="Pharaoh 90">
        Pharaoh 90
       </a>
       to Earth to merge with the planet. Tomoe's daughter Hotaru is possessed by the entity
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mistress_9" title="Mistress 9">
        Mistress 9
       </a>
       , who must open the dimensional gateway through which Pharaoh 90 must travel. Auto-racer Haruka Tenoh and violinist Michiru Kaioh appear as
       <a href="/wiki/Sailor_Uranus" title="Sailor Uranus">
        Sailor Uranus
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Sailor_Neptune" title="Sailor Neptune">
        Sailor Neptune
       </a>
       , who guard the outer rim of the Solar System from external threats. Physics student Setsuna Meioh, Sailor Pluto's reincarnation, joins the protagonists. Usagi obtains the Holy Grail, transforms into Super Sailor Moon, and attempts to use the power of the Grail and the Silver Crystal to destroy Pharaoh 90. This causes Hotaru to awaken as
       <a href="/wiki/Sailor_Saturn" title="Sailor Saturn">
        Sailor Saturn
       </a>
       , whom Haruka, Michiru and Setsuna initially perceive as a threat. As the harbinger of death, Hotaru uses her power of destruction to sever Pharaoh 90 from the Earth and instructs Setsuna to use her power over time-space to close the dimensional gateway.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the fourth arc, Usagi and her friends enter high school and fight against the
       <a href="/wiki/Dead_Moon_Circus" title="Dead Moon Circus">
        Dead Moon Circus
       </a>
       , led by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen_Nehelenia" title="Queen Nehelenia">
        Queen Nehelenia
       </a>
       , the self-proclaimed "rightful ruler" of both Silver Millennium and Earth. Nehelenia invades Elysion, which hosts the Earth's Golden Kingdom, capturing its High Priest
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Helios/Pegasus" title="Helios/Pegasus">
        Helios
       </a>
       and instructs her followers to steal the Silver Crystal. As Prince Endymion, Mamoru is revealed to be the owner of the Golden Crystal—the sacred stone of the Golden Kingdom. Mamoru and the Soldiers combine their powers with those of the Holy Grail, enabling Usagi to transform into Eternal Sailor Moon and kill Nehelenia. Four of Nehelenia's henchmen, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amazoness_Quartet" title="Amazoness Quartet">
        Amazoness Quartet
       </a>
       , are revealed to be Sailor Soldiers called the Sailor Quartet, who are destined to become Chibiusa's guardians in the future; they had been awakened prematurely and corrupted by Nehelenia.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the final arc, Usagi and her friends are drawn into a battle against
       <a href="/wiki/Shadow_Galactica" title="Shadow Galactica">
        Shadow Galactica
       </a>
       , a group of false Sailor Soldiers. Their leader
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sailor_Galaxia" title="Sailor Galaxia">
        Sailor Galaxia
       </a>
       plans to steal the Sailor Crystals of true Soldiers to take over the galaxy and kill an evil lifeform known as Chaos. After killing Mamoru and most of the Sailor Soldiers, Sailor Galaxia steals their Sailor Crystals. Usagi travels to the Galaxy Cauldron to defeat Galaxia and revive her teammates. Joining Usagi are the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sailor_Starlights" title="Sailor Starlights">
        Sailor Starlights
       </a>
       who come from the planet Kinmoku, their ruler
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Princess_Kakyuu" title="Princess Kakyuu">
        Princess Kakyuu
       </a>
       and the infant
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chibichibi" title="Chibichibi">
        Sailor Chibichibi
       </a>
       who comes from the distant future. Later, Chibiusa and the Sailor Quartet join Usagi and company. After numerous battles and the death of Galaxia, Sailor Chibichibi reveals her true form as Sailor Cosmos. After Usagi destroys Chaos with the Silver Crystal, she revives Mamoru and the Sailor Soldiers, before returning to Earth. The series ends with Usagi and Mamoru's wedding six years later.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Production">
        Production
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sailor_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Production">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Naoko_Takeuchi" title="Naoko Takeuchi">
        Naoko Takeuchi
       </a>
       redeveloped
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       from her 1991 manga serial
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Codename:_Sailor_V" title="Codename: Sailor V">
         Codename: Sailor V
        </a>
       </i>
       , which was first published on August 20, 1991, and featured Sailor Venus as the main protagonist.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timeline_6-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-timeline-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Takeuchi wanted to create a story with a theme about girls in outer space. While discussing with her editor Fumio Osano, he suggested the addition of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sailor_fuku" title="Sailor fuku">
        Sailor fuku
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shinzōban2back_7-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-shinzōban2back-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       When
       <i>
        Codename: Sailor V
       </i>
       was proposed for adaptation into an anime by
       <a href="/wiki/Toei_Animation" title="Toei Animation">
        Toei Animation
       </a>
       , Takeuchi redeveloped the concept so Sailor Venus became a member of a team.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The resulting manga series became a fusion of the popular magical girl genre and the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Super_Sentai" title="Super Sentai">
         Super Sentai
        </a>
       </i>
       series, of which Takeuchi was a fan.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCarter_10-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-McCarter-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Recurring motifs include astronomy,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shinzōban2back_7-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-shinzōban2back-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       astrology, Greek and Roman mythology,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grigsby_11-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Grigsby-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       geology,
       <a href="/wiki/Classical_element" title="Classical element">
        Japanese elemental themes
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drazen_12-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Drazen-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         286
        </span>
       </sup>
       teen fashions,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grigsby_11-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Grigsby-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allison_13-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Allison-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and schoolgirl antics.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allison_13-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Allison-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Takeuchi said discussions with Kodansha originally envisaged a single story arc;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Materials_14-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Materials-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the storyline was developed in meetings a year before serialization began.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schodt_15-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Schodt-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         93
        </span>
       </sup>
       After completing the arc, Toei and Kodansha asked Takeuchi to continue the series. She wrote four more story arcs,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Materials_14-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Materials-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which were often published simultaneously with the five corresponding seasons of the anime adaptation. The anime ran one or two months behind the manga.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schodt_15-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Schodt-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         93
        </span>
       </sup>
       As a result, the anime follows the storyline of the manga fairly closely, although there are deviations.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MTV_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-MTV-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Takeuchi later said because Toei's production staff were mostly male, she feels the anime has "a slight male perspective."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MTV_16-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-MTV-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Takeuchi later said she planned to kill off the protagonists, but Osano rejected the notion and said, "[
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       ] is a
       <a href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Djo_manga" title="Shōjo manga">
        shōjo manga
       </a>
       !" When the anime adaptation was produced, the protagonists were killed in the final battle with the Dark Kingdom, although they were revived. Takeuchi resented that she was unable to do that in her version.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vol3_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-vol3-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Takeuchi also intended for the
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       anime adaptation to last for one season, but due to the immense popularity, Toei asked Takeuchi to continue the series. At first, she struggled to develop another storyline to extend the series. While discussing with Osano, he suggested the inclusion of Usagi's daughter from the future, Chibiusa.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vol3_17-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-vol3-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Media">
        Media
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sailor_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Media">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Manga">
        Manga
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sailor_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Manga">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Sailor_Moon_chapters" title="List of Sailor Moon chapters">
        List of Sailor Moon chapters
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Written and illustrated by
       <a href="/wiki/Naoko_Takeuchi" title="Naoko Takeuchi">
        Naoko Takeuchi
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       was serialized in the monthly manga anthology
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Nakayoshi" title="Nakayoshi">
         Nakayoshi
        </a>
       </i>
       from December 28, 1991 to February 3, 1997.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timeline_6-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-timeline-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The side-stories were serialized simultaneously in
       <i>
        RunRun
       </i>
       —another of Kodansha's manga magazines.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timeline_6-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-timeline-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The 52 individual chapters were published in 18
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Tank%C5%8Dbon" title="Tankōbon">
         tankōbon
        </a>
       </i>
       volumes by
       <a href="/wiki/Kodansha" title="Kodansha">
        Kodansha
       </a>
       from July 6, 1992, to April 4, 1997.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JPVolume1_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-JPVolume1-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JPVolume18_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-JPVolume18-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2003, the chapters were re-released in a collection of 12
       <i>
        shinzōban
       </i>
       volumes to coincide with the release of the
       <a href="/wiki/Pretty_Guardian_Sailor_Moon_(live-action_series)" title="Pretty Guardian Sailor Moon (live-action series)">
        live-action series
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SMShinsoVolume1_20-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-SMShinsoVolume1-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The manga was retitled
       <i>
        Pretty Guardian Sailor Moon
       </i>
       and included new cover art,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and revised dialogue and illustrations. The ten individual short stories were also released in 2 volumes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2013, the chapters were once again re-released in 10
       <i>
        kanzenban
       </i>
       volumes to commemorate the manga's 20th anniversary, which includes digitally remastered artwork, new covers and color artwork from its
       <i>
        Nakayoshi
       </i>
       run.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-完全版（１）_24-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-完全版（１）-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The books have been enlarged from the typical Japanese manga size to A5.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-missdream_26-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-missdream-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The short stories were republished in two volumes, with the order of the stories shuffled.
       <i>
        Codename: Sailor V
       </i>
       was also included in the third edition.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-missdream_26-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-missdream-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       manga was initially licensed for an English release by Mixx (later
       <a href="/wiki/Tokyopop" title="Tokyopop">
        Tokyopop
       </a>
       ) in North America. The manga was first published as a serial in
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TOKYOPOP_(magazine)" title="TOKYOPOP (magazine)">
         MixxZine
        </a>
       </i>
       beginning in 1997, but was later removed from the magazine and made into a separate, monthly comic to finish the first, second and third arcs. At the same time, the fourth and fifth arcs were printed in a secondary magazine called
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Smile_(magazine)" title="Smile (magazine)">
         Smile
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The series was later collected into three-part graphic novels spanning eighteen volumes, which were published from December 1, 1998, to September 18, 2001.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TokyopopUSVolume1_28-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-TokyopopUSVolume1-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TokyopopUSVolume18_29-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-TokyopopUSVolume18-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Tokyopop's license expired in 2005 and its edition went out of print.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Daily pages from the Tokyopop version ran in the Japanimation Station, a service accessible to users of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/America_Online" title="America Online">
        America Online
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In May 2005, Tokyopop's license to the Sailor Moon manga expired, and its edition went out of print.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2011,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kodansha_Comics" title="Kodansha Comics">
        Kodansha Comics
       </a>
       announced it would publish the
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       manga and the lead-in series
       <i>
        Codename: Sailor V
       </i>
       in English.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kodansha2011English_33-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kodansha2011English-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It would also re-publish the twelve volumes of
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       simultaneously with the two-volume edition of
       <i>
        Codename Sailor V
       </i>
       , from September 2011 to July 2013.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The first volume of the two related short stories was published on September 10, 2013;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the other was published on November 26.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The manga has also been licensed in other English-speaking countries. In the United Kingdom, the volumes are distributed by Turnaround Publisher Services.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In Australia, the manga is distributed by Random House Australia.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Anime_series">
        Anime series
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sailor_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Anime series">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sailor_Moon">
        <i>
         Sailor Moon
        </i>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sailor_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Sailor Moon">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Sailor_Moon_(anime)" title="Sailor Moon (anime)">
        Sailor Moon (anime)
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Sailor_Moon_episodes" title="List of Sailor Moon episodes">
        List of Sailor Moon episodes
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Toei Animation produced an anime television series based on the 52 manga chapters, also titled
       <i>
        Pretty Soldier Sailor Moon
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DVD‐COLLECTION_Vol.1_4-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-DVD‐COLLECTION_Vol.1-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DVD‐COLLECTION_Vol.2_5-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-DVD‐COLLECTION_Vol.2-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was directed by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Junichi_Sat%C5%8D" title="Junichi Satō">
        Junichi Satō
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Kunihiko_Ikuhara" title="Kunihiko Ikuhara">
        Kunihiko Ikuhara
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Takuya_Igarashi" title="Takuya Igarashi">
        Takuya Igarashi
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doi_41-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Doi-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The series premiered in Japan on
       <a href="/wiki/TV_Asahi" title="TV Asahi">
        TV Asahi
       </a>
       on March 7, 1992, and ran for 200 episodes until its conclusion on February 8, 1997. Most of the international versions, including the English adaptations, are titled
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sailor_Moon_Crystal">
        <i>
         Sailor Moon Crystal
        </i>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sailor_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Sailor Moon Crystal">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Sailor_Moon_Crystal" title="Sailor Moon Crystal">
        Sailor Moon Crystal
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Sailor_Moon_Crystal_episodes" title="List of Sailor Moon Crystal episodes">
        List of Sailor Moon Crystal episodes
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       On July 6, 2012, Kodansha and Toei Animation announced that it would commence production of a new anime adaptation of
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       , called
       <i>
        Sailor Moon Crystal
       </i>
       , for a simultaneous worldwide release in 2013 as part of the series' 20th anniversary celebrations.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20th_42-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-20th-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Japan_Times_44-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Japan_Times-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        Crystal
       </i>
       premiered on July 5, 2014, and episodes would premiere on the first and third Saturdays of each month.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smcrystalcast_46-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-smcrystalcast-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Kotono_Mitsuishi" title="Kotono Mitsuishi">
        Kotono Mitsuishi
       </a>
       reprised her role as Sailor Moon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The first two seasons were released together, covering their corresponding arcs of the manga ("Dark Kingdom" and "Black Moon"). A third season (subtitled "Death Busters" based on the "Infinity" arc on the manga) premiered on April 4, 2016.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On January 25, 2017, another sequel was announced.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Films_and_television_specials">
        Films and television specials
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sailor_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Films and television specials">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Three animated theatrical feature films based on the original
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       series have been released in Japan. The films are side-stories that do not correlate with the timeline of the original series. A one-hour television special was aired on TV Asahi in Japan on April 8, 1995.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1997, an article in
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Variety_(magazine)" title="Variety (magazine)">
         Variety
        </a>
       </i>
       stated that
       <a href="/wiki/The_Walt_Disney_Company" title="The Walt Disney Company">
        The Walt Disney Company
       </a>
       was interested in acquiring the rights to
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       as a live action film to be directed by
       <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Tong" title="Stanley Tong">
        Stanley Tong
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Companion_books">
        Companion books
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sailor_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Companion books">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       There have been numerous companion books to
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       . Kodansha released some of these books for each of the five story arcs, collectively called the
       <i>
        Original Picture Collection
       </i>
       . The books contain cover art, promotional material and other work by Takeuchi. Many of the drawings are accompanied by comments on the way she developed her ideas, created each picture and commentary on the anime interpretation of her story.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Takeuchi_1-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Takeuchi-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Another picture collection,
       <i>
        Volume Infinity
       </i>
       , was released as a self-published, limited-edition artbook after the end of the series in 1997. This artbook includes drawings by Takeuchi and her friends, her staff, and many of the voice actors who worked on the anime. In 1999, Kodansha published the
       <i>
        Materials Collection
       </i>
       ; this contained development sketches and notes for nearly every character in the manga, and for some characters that never appeared. Each drawing includes notes by Takeuchi about costume pieces, the mentality of the characters and her feelings about them. It also includes timelines for the story arcs and for the real-life release of products and materials relating to the anime and manga. A short story,
       <i>
        Parallel Sailor Moon
       </i>
       is also featured, celebrating the
       <a href="/wiki/Rabbit_(zodiac)" title="Rabbit (zodiac)">
        year of the rabbit
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Materials_14-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Materials-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Stage_musicals">
        Stage musicals
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sailor_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Stage musicals">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Sailor_Moon_musicals" title="Sailor Moon musicals">
        Sailor Moon musicals
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In mid-1993, the first musical theater production based on
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       premiered;
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anza_Ohyama" title="Anza Ohyama">
        Anza Ohyama
       </a>
       starred as Sailor Moon. Thirty such musicals in all have been produced, with one in pre-production. The shows' stories include anime-inspired plotlines and original material. Music from the series has been released on about 20 memorial albums.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The popularity of the musicals has been cited as a reason behind the production of the live action television series,
       <i>
        Pretty Guardian Sailor Moon
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PGSMAF_57-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-PGSMAF-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       During the original run musicals ran in the winter and summer of each year, with summer musicals staged at the
       <a href="/wiki/Sunshine_City,_Tokyo" title="Sunshine City, Tokyo">
        Sunshine
       </a>
       Theater in the
       <a href="/wiki/Ikebukuro" title="Ikebukuro">
        Ikebukuro
       </a>
       area of Tokyo. In the winter, musicals toured to other large cities in Japan, including
       <a href="/wiki/Osaka" title="Osaka">
        Osaka
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Fukuoka" title="Fukuoka">
        Fukuoka
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
        <a href="#cite_note-58">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Nagoya" title="Nagoya">
        Nagoya
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Shizuoka,_Shizuoka" title="Shizuoka, Shizuoka">
        Shizuoka
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kanazawa,_Ishikawa" title="Kanazawa, Ishikawa">
        Kanazawa
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sendai" title="Sendai">
        Sendai
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
        <a href="#cite_note-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Saga,_Saga" title="Saga, Saga">
        Saga
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/%C5%8Cita,_%C5%8Cita" title="Ōita, Ōita">
        Oita
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Yamagata,_Yamagata" title="Yamagata, Yamagata">
        Yamagata
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Fukushima,_Fukushima" title="Fukushima, Fukushima">
        Fukushima
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The final incarnation of the first run,
       <i>
        New Legend of Kaguya Island (Revised Edition)
       </i>
       <span style="font-weight: normal">
        (
        <span lang="ja">
         新・かぐや島伝説 &lt;改訂版&gt;
        </span>
        <span style="display:none">
         ,
        </span>
        <i>
         Shin Kaguyashima Densetsu (Kaiteban)
        </i>
        )
       </span>
       , went on stage in January 2005, following which,
       <a href="/wiki/Bandai" title="Bandai">
        Bandai
       </a>
       officially put the series on a hiatus.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
        <a href="#cite_note-61">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On June 2, 2013, Fumio Osano announced on his Twitter page that the
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       musicals would begin again in September 2013.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
        <a href="#cite_note-62">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The 20th anniversary show
       <i>
        La Reconquista
       </i>
       ran from September 13 to 23 at Shibuya's AiiA Theater Tokyo, with Satomi Ōkubo as Sailor Moon. Satomi Ōkubo reprised the role in the 2014 production
       <i>
        Petite Étrangère
       </i>
       which ran from August 21 to September 7, 2014, again at AiiA Theater Tokyo.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Live-action_series">
        Live-action series
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sailor_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Live-action series">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="American_remake">
        American remake
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sailor_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: American remake">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In 1993, Renaissance-Atlantic Entertainment, Bandai and Toon Makers, Inc. conceptualized their own version of
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       , which was half live-action and half Western-style animation. Toon Makers produced a 17-minute
       <a href="/wiki/Proof_of_concept" title="Proof of concept">
        proof of concept
       </a>
       presentation video as well as a two-minute music video, both of which were directed by Rocky Sotoloff, for this concept. Renaissance-Atlantic presented the concept to Toei, but it was turned down as their concept would have cost significantly more than simply exporting and dubbing the anime adaptation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saban_Moon_63-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Saban_Moon-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources">
          <span title="The material near this tag relies on an unreliable source. Source is a self-published site. (December 2017)">
           unreliable source
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       At the 1998
       <a href="/wiki/Anime_Expo" title="Anime Expo">
        Anime Expo
       </a>
       convention in Los Angeles, the music video was shown. It has since been copied numerous times and has been viewed on many streaming video sites. Because of the relatively poor quality of the source video and circulated footage, many anime fans thought that the music video was actually a leaked trailer for the project.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research">
          <span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (March 2016)">
           original research?
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       Additional copies of the footage have since been uploaded to the Internet and served only to bolster the mistaken assumption, in addition to incorrectly citing the production to
       <a href="/wiki/Saban_Entertainment" title="Saban Entertainment">
        Saban Entertainment
       </a>
       , who became known for a similar treatment that created the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Power_Rangers" title="Power Rangers">
         Power Rangers
        </a>
       </i>
       series.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saban_Moon_63-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Saban_Moon-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Pretty_Guardian_Sailor_Moon">
        <i>
         Pretty Guardian Sailor Moon
        </i>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sailor_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Pretty Guardian Sailor Moon">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Pretty_Guardian_Sailor_Moon_(live-action_series)" title="Pretty Guardian Sailor Moon (live-action series)">
        Pretty Guardian Sailor Moon (live-action series)
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In 2003,
       <a href="/wiki/Toei_Company" title="Toei Company">
        Toei Company
       </a>
       produced a Japanese live-action
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       television series using the new translated English title of
       <i>
        Pretty Guardian Sailor Moon
       </i>
       . Its 49 episodes were broadcast on
       <a href="/wiki/Chubu-Nippon_Broadcasting" title="Chubu-Nippon Broadcasting">
        Chubu-Nippon Broadcasting
       </a>
       from October 4, 2003 to September 25, 2004.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        Pretty Guardian Sailor Moon
       </i>
       featured
       <a href="/wiki/Miyuu_Sawai" title="Miyuu Sawai">
        Miyuu Sawai
       </a>
       as Usagi Tsukino,
       <a href="/wiki/Rika_Izumi" title="Rika Izumi">
        Rika Izumi
       </a>
       (credited as Chisaki Hama) as Ami Mizuno,
       <a href="/wiki/Keiko_Kitagawa" title="Keiko Kitagawa">
        Keiko Kitagawa
       </a>
       as Rei Hino,
       <a href="/wiki/Mew_Azama" title="Mew Azama">
        Mew Azama
       </a>
       as Makoto Kino,
       <a href="/wiki/Ayaka_Komatsu" title="Ayaka Komatsu">
        Ayaka Komatsu
       </a>
       as Minako Aino,
       <a href="/wiki/Jouji_Shibue" title="Jouji Shibue">
        Jouji Shibue
       </a>
       as Mamoru Chiba,
       <a href="/wiki/Keiko_Han" title="Keiko Han">
        Keiko Han
       </a>
       reprising her voice role as Luna from the original anime and
       <a href="/wiki/Kappei_Yamaguchi" title="Kappei Yamaguchi">
        Kappei Yamaguchi
       </a>
       voicing Artemis. The series was an alternate retelling of the Dark Kingdom arc, adding a storyline different from that in the manga and first anime series, with original characters and new plot developments.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PGSMAF_57-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-PGSMAF-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PGSM_ANN_66-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-PGSM_ANN-66">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In addition to the main episodes, two
       <a href="/wiki/Direct-to-video" title="Direct-to-video">
        direct-to-video
       </a>
       releases appeared after the show ended its television broadcast. "Special Act" is set four years after the main storyline ends, and shows the wedding of the two main characters. "Act Zero" is a prequel showing the origins of
       <a href="/wiki/Codename:_Sailor_V" title="Codename: Sailor V">
        Sailor V
       </a>
       and Tuxedo Mask.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
        <a href="#cite_note-67">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Video_games">
        Video games
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sailor_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Video games">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Sailor_Moon_video_games" title="List of Sailor Moon video games">
        List of Sailor Moon video games
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       franchise has spawned several video games across various genres and platforms. Most were made by Bandai and its subsidy Angel; others were produced by
       <a href="/wiki/Banpresto" title="Banpresto">
        Banpresto
       </a>
       . The early games were
       <a href="/wiki/Beat_%27em_up" title="Beat 'em up">
        side-scrolling fighters
       </a>
       ; later ones were unique
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computer_puzzle_game" title="Computer puzzle game">
        puzzle games
       </a>
       , or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Versus_fighting_game" title="Versus fighting game">
        versus fighting games
       </a>
       .
       <i>
        Another Story
       </i>
       was a turn-based
       <a href="/wiki/Role-playing_video_game" title="Role-playing video game">
        role-playing video game
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gamesearch_68-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-gamesearch-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The only
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       game produced outside Japan, 3VR New Media's
       <i>
        The 3D Adventures of Sailor Moon
       </i>
       , went on sale in North America in 1997.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A video game called
       <i>
        Sailor Moon: La Luna Splende
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        Sailor Moon: The Shining Moon
       </i>
       ) was released on March 16, 2011 for the
       <a href="/wiki/Nintendo_DS" title="Nintendo DS">
        Nintendo DS
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
        <a href="#cite_note-70">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Reception">
        Reception
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sailor_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Reception">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       is one of the most popular manga series of all time and continues to enjoy high readership worldwide. More than one million copies of its
       <i>
        tankōbon
       </i>
       volumes had been sold in Japan by the end of 1995.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schodt_15-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Schodt-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         95
        </span>
       </sup>
       By the series' 20th anniversary in 2012, this number had grown to 35 million copies in over fifty countries.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
        <a href="#cite_note-71">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The manga won the
       <a href="/wiki/Kodansha_Manga_Award" title="Kodansha Manga Award">
        Kodansha Manga Award
       </a>
       in 1993 for
       <i>
        shōjo
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
        <a href="#cite_note-72">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The English adaptations of both the manga and the anime series became the first successful
       <a href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Djo" title="Shōjo">
        shōjo
       </a>
       title in the United States.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson_73-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Thompson-73">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The character of Sailor Moon is recognized as one of the most important and popular
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Female_superheroes" title="Female superheroes">
        female superheroes
       </a>
       of all time.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
        <a href="#cite_note-74">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
        <a href="#cite_note-75">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
        <a href="#cite_note-76">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
        <a href="#cite_note-77">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       has also become popular internationally.
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       was broadcast in Spain and France beginning in December 1993; these became the first countries outside Japan to broadcast the series.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SMhist93_78-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-SMhist93-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was later aired in Russia, South Korea, the Philippines, China, Italy, Taiwan, Thailand, Indonesia and Hong Kong, before North America picked up the franchise for adaptation. In the Philippines,
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       was one of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Associated_Broadcasting_Company" title="Associated Broadcasting Company">
        its carrier network
       </a>
       's main draws, helping it to become the third-biggest network in the country.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drazen_12-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Drazen-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         10-11
        </span>
       </sup>
       In 2001, the
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       manga was Tokyopop's best selling property, outselling the next-best selling titles by at least a factor of 1.5.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
        <a href="#cite_note-79">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Diamond_Comic_Distributors" title="Diamond Comic Distributors">
        Diamond Comic Distributors
       </a>
       's May 1999 "Graphic Novel and Trade Paperback" category,
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       Volume 3 was the best-selling comic book in the United States.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
        <a href="#cite_note-80">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In his 2007 book
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Manga:_The_Complete_Guide" title="Manga: The Complete Guide">
         Manga: The Complete Guide
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Jason_Thompson_(writer)" title="Jason Thompson (writer)">
        Jason Thompson
       </a>
       gave the manga series three stars out of four. He enjoyed the blending of
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Dnen_manga" title="Shōnen manga">
         shōnen
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        shōjo
       </i>
       styles, and said the combat scenes seemed heavily influenced by
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Saint_Seiya" title="Saint Seiya">
         Saint Seiya
        </a>
       </i>
       , but shorter and less bloody. He also said the manga itself appeared similar to
       <i>
        Super Sentai
       </i>
       television shows. Thompson found the series fun and entertaining, but said the repetitive plot lines were a detriment to the title, which the increasing quality of art could not make up for; even so, he called the series "sweet, effective entertainment."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson_73-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Thompson-73">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Thompson said although the audience for
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       is both male and female, Takeuchi does not use excessive
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fanservice" title="Fanservice">
        fanservice
       </a>
       for males, which would run the risk of alienating her female audience. Thompson said fight scenes are not physical and "boil down to their purest form of a clash of wills", which he says "makes thematic sense" for the manga.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
        <a href="#cite_note-81">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Comparing the manga and anime, Sylvain Durand said the manga artwork is "gorgeous", but its storytelling is more compressed and erratic and the anime has more character development. Durand said "the sense of tragedy is greater" in the manga's telling of the "fall of the Silver Millennium," giving more detail about the origins of the Shitennou and on Usagi's final battle with Beryl and Metaria. Durand said the anime omits information that makes the story easy to understand, but judges the anime more "coherent" with a better balance of comedy and tragedy, whereas the manga is "more tragic" and focused on Usagi and Mamoru's romance.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
        <a href="#cite_note-82">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       For the week of September 11, 2011, to September 17, 2011, the first volume of the re-released
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       manga was the best-selling manga on
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_The_New_York_Times_Manga_Best_Sellers" title="List of The New York Times Manga Best Sellers">
        <i>
         The New York Times
        </i>
        Manga Best Sellers list
       </a>
       , with the first volume of
       <i>
        Codename: Sailor V
       </i>
       in second place.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
        <a href="#cite_note-83">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
        <a href="#cite_note-84">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The first print run of the first volume sold out after four weeks.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
        <a href="#cite_note-85">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">
        Legacy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sailor_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Legacy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       With their dynamic heroines and action-oriented plots, many attribute the manga and anime series to reinvigorating the magical girl genre. After its success, many similar magical girl series, including
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Magic_Knight_Rayearth" title="Magic Knight Rayearth">
         Magic Knight Rayearth
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wedding_Peach" title="Wedding Peach">
         Wedding Peach
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Nurse_Angel_Ririka_SOS" title="Nurse Angel Ririka SOS">
         Nurse Angel Ririka SOS
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Pretty_Cure" title="Pretty Cure">
         Pretty Cure
        </a>
       </i>
       , emerged.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson_73-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Thompson-73">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         199
        </span>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poitras_86-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Poitras-86">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       has been called "the biggest breakthrough" in English-dubbed anime until 1995, when it premiered on
       <a href="/wiki/YTV_(TV_channel)" title="YTV (TV channel)">
        YTV
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drazen_12-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Drazen-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         10-11
        </span>
       </sup>
       and "the pinnacle of little kid
       <i>
        shōjo
       </i>
       anime."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
        <a href="#cite_note-87">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Cultural anthropologist
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Matt_Thorn" title="Matt Thorn">
        Matt Thorn
       </a>
       said that soon after
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        shōjo
       </i>
       manga started appearing in book shops instead of fandom-dominated comic shops.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PW_Matt_Thorn_88-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-PW_Matt_Thorn-88">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The series are credited as beginning a wider movement of girls taking up
       <i>
        shōjo
       </i>
       manga.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson_73-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Thompson-73">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
        <a href="#cite_note-89">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Canadian librarian
       <a href="/wiki/Gilles_Poitras" title="Gilles Poitras">
        Gilles Poitras
       </a>
       defines a generation of anime fans as those who were introduced to anime by
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       in the 1990s, saying they were both much younger than other fans and were also mostly female.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poitras_86-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Poitras-86">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Historian
       <a href="/wiki/Fred_Patten" title="Fred Patten">
        Fred Patten
       </a>
       credits Takeuchi with popularizing the concept of a
       <i>
        Super Sentai
       </i>
       -like team of magical girls,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ST_90-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ST-90">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patten_91-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Patten-91">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and Paul Gravett credits the series with revitalizing the magical girl genre itself.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gravett_92-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gravett-92">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A reviewer for
       <i>
        <abbr>
         THEM
        </abbr>
        Anime Reviews
       </i>
       also credited the anime series with changing the genre—its heroine must use her powers to fight evil, not simply have fun as previous magical girls had done.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-THEM_93-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-THEM-93">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Sailor Moon also influenced the development of
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Powerpuff_Girls" title="Powerpuff Girls">
         Powerpuff Girls
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Totally_Spies!" title="Totally Spies!">
         Totally Spies!
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
        <a href="#cite_note-94">
         [94]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">
        western culture
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       is sometimes associated with the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feminist" title="Feminist">
        feminist
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Girl_Power" title="Girl Power">
        Girl Power
       </a>
       movements and with empowering its viewers,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
        <a href="#cite_note-95">
         [95]
        </a>
       </sup>
       especially regarding the "credible, charismatic and independent" characterizations of the Sailor Soldiers, which were "interpreted in France as an unambiguously feminist position".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dicomanga_96-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Dicomanga-96">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Although
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       is regarded as empowering to women and feminism in concept, through the aggressive nature and strong personalities of the Sailor Soldiers,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FEMSPEC_97-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FEMSPEC-97">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
       it is a specific type of feminist concept where "traditional feminine ideals [are] incorporated into characters that act in traditionally male capacities".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FEMSPEC_97-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-FEMSPEC-97">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
       While the Sailor Soldiers are strong, independent fighters who thwart evil—which is generally a masculine stereotype—they are also ideally feminized in the transformation of the Sailor Soldiers from teenage girls into magical girls, with heavy emphasis on jewelry, make-up and their highly sexualized outfits with cleavage, short skirts and accentuated waists.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grigsby_11-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Grigsby-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The most notable hyper-feminine features of the Sailor Soldiers—and most other females in Japanese girls' comics—are the girls' thin bodies, long legs, and, in particular, round, orb-like eyes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grigsby_11-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Grigsby-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Eyes are commonly known as the primal source within characters where emotion is evoked—sensitive characters have larger eyes than insensitive ones.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FEMSPEC_97-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-FEMSPEC-97">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Male characters generally have smaller eyes that have no sparkle or shine in them like the eyes of the female characters.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FEMSPEC_97-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-FEMSPEC-97">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The stereotypical role of women in Japanese culture is to undertake romantic and loving feelings;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grigsby_11-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Grigsby-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       therefore, the prevalence of hyper-feminine qualities like the openness of the female eye in Japanese girls' comics is clearly exhibited in
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       . Thus,
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       emphasizes a type of feminist model by combining traditional masculine action with traditional female affection and sexuality through the Sailor Soldiers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FEMSPEC_97-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-FEMSPEC-97">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Its characters are often described with "catty stereotypes", Sailor Moon's character in particular being singled out as less than feminist.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Internet_Moonies_98-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Internet_Moonies-98">
         [98]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       has also been compared to
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Mighty_Morphin_Power_Rangers" title="Mighty Morphin Power Rangers">
         Mighty Morphin Power Rangers
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grigsby_11-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Grigsby-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Craig_99-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Craig-99">
         [99]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buffy_the_Vampire_Slayer_(TV_series)" title="Buffy the Vampire Slayer (TV series)">
         Buffy the Vampire Slayer
        </a>
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drazen_12-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Drazen-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         281
        </span>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
        <a href="#cite_note-100">
         [100]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
        <a href="#cite_note-101">
         [101]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sabrina,_the_Teenage_Witch_(TV_series)" title="Sabrina, the Teenage Witch (TV series)">
         Sabrina, the Teenage Witch
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
        <a href="#cite_note-102">
         [102]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       James Welker said
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       <span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">
        '
       </span>
       s futuristic setting helps to make lesbianism "naturalized" and a peaceful existence.
       <a href="/wiki/Yukari_Fujimoto" title="Yukari Fujimoto">
        Yukari Fujimoto
       </a>
       said although there are few "lesbian scenes" in
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       , it has become a popular subject for
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Yuri_(genre)" title="Yuri (genre)">
         yuri
        </a>
       </i>
       <a href="/wiki/D%C5%8Djinshi" title="Dōjinshi">
        dōjinshi
       </a>
       . She cites this to the source work's "cheerful" tone, although she says "though they seem to be overflowing with lesbians, the position of heterosexuals is earnestly secured."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
        <a href="#cite_note-103">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In English-speaking countries,
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       developed a cult following among anime fans and male university students.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grigsby_11-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-Grigsby-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Patrick Drazen says the
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_World_Wide_Web" title="History of the World Wide Web">
        Internet
       </a>
       was a new medium that fans used to communicate and played a role in the popularity of
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drazen_12-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Drazen-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         281
        </span>
       </sup>
       Fans could use the Internet to communicate about the series, organize campaigns to return
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       to U.S. broadcast, to share information about episodes that had not yet aired, or to write
       <a href="/wiki/Fan_fiction" title="Fan fiction">
        fan fiction
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Internet_Moonies_98-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Internet_Moonies-98">
         [98]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
        <a href="#cite_note-104">
         [104]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2004, one study said there were 3,335,000 websites about Sailor Moon, compared to 491,000 for
       <a href="/wiki/Mickey_Mouse" title="Mickey Mouse">
        Mickey Mouse
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">
        <a href="#cite_note-105">
         [105]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Gemma Cox of
       <a href="/wiki/Neo_(magazine)" title="Neo (magazine)">
        <i>
         Neo
        </i>
        magazine
       </a>
       said part of the series' allure was that fans communicated via the Internet about the differences between the dub and the original version.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106">
        <a href="#cite_note-106">
         [106]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       fandom was described in 1997 as being "small and dispersed."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107">
        <a href="#cite_note-107">
         [107]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In a United States study, twelve children paid rapt attention to the fighting scenes in
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       , although when asked whether they thought
       <i>
        Sailor Moon
       </i>
       was violent, only two said yes and the other ten described the episodes as "soft" or "cute."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108">
        <a href="#cite_note-108">
         [108]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
